  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_cead3dcb-91b1-45c6-8684-219b48318cff'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer14668852939683745965">Alistair MacLeod musters all of the skill and grace that have won him an international following to give us <em>No Great Mischief</em>, the story of a fiercely loyal family and the tradition that drives it.<br/><br/><br/><br/>Generations after their forebears went into exile, the MacDonalds still face seemingly unmitigated hardships and cruelties of life. Alexander, orphaned as a child by a horrific t</span>
  <span id="freeText14668852939683745965" style="display:none">Alistair MacLeod musters all of the skill and grace that have won him an international following to give us <em>No Great Mischief</em>, the story of a fiercely loyal family and the tradition that drives it.<br /><br /><br /><br />Generations after their forebears went into exile, the MacDonalds still face seemingly unmitigated hardships and cruelties of life. Alexander, orphaned as a child by a horrific tragedy, has nevertheless gained some success in the world. Even his older brother, Calum, a nearly destitute alcoholic living on Toronto's skid row, has been scarred by another tragedy. But, like all his clansman, Alexander is sustained by a family history that seems to run through his veins. And through these lovingly recounted stories-wildly comic or heartbreakingly tragic-we discover the hope against hope upon which every family must sometimes rely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14668852939683745965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/4501769-sandy"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1290120351i/4501769._UY100_CR25,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/4501769-sandy">Sandy</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Earlier in the book, when they bring the American-draft-dodger Alexander to the miner's camp, he is wearing a Celtic ring, saying his grandfather gave<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Earlier in the book, when they bring the American-draft-dodger Alexander to the miner's camp, he is wearing a Celtic ring, saying his grandfather gave it to him, and that it represents "the never-ending circle." So Calum wearing the same design ring and returning to Cape Breton represents a part of their family's never-ending circle of relationships, all of which seem to return to that land at Cape Breton that connects them all as a clan.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3489354811767145480">All this obsession with the family past and a wish to get down to the roots...<br/><i>No Great Mischief</i> seems to be too ordinary to win so many prestigious literary awards and though I liked the novel I wasn't impressed greatly.<br/><blockquote>In the landscape around me, those who harvest the bounty of the earth are stilled for the day. Yet they are there in the near-darkness with their own hopes and dreams and disappointments.</blockquote><br/>At first there are great expectations then there are just hopes and then one keeps hoping aga</span>
  <span id="freeText3489354811767145480" style="display:none">All this obsession with the family past and a wish to get down to the roots...<br /><i>No Great Mischief</i> seems to be too ordinary to win so many prestigious literary awards and though I liked the novel I wasn't impressed greatly.<br /><blockquote>In the landscape around me, those who harvest the bounty of the earth are stilled for the day. Yet they are there in the near-darkness with their own hopes and dreams and disappointments.</blockquote><br />At first there are great expectations then there are just hopes and then one keeps hoping against hope...</span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375726651" href="/book/show/14273.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2860054898637841470"><i>April 21, 2014: Rest in peace, Alistair MacLeod. Died April 20, 2014.<br/>His extraordinary style will never be matched.</i> <br/><br/>Another outstanding piece of storytelling from this great Canadian writer. He uses repetition of images and phrases throughout the book as a very effective tool. It gives the story both a rhythm and an anchor, continually bringing you back to reminders of what binds the clan and their shared history.<br/><br/>This is the story of the Scottish clan of Calum the Red, who came to Nova Scotia ov</span>
  <span id="freeText2860054898637841470" style="display:none"><i>April 21, 2014: Rest in peace, Alistair MacLeod. Died April 20, 2014.<br />His extraordinary style will never be matched.</i> <br /><br />Another outstanding piece of storytelling from this great Canadian writer. He uses repetition of images and phrases throughout the book as a very effective tool. It gives the story both a rhythm and an anchor, continually bringing you back to reminders of what binds the clan and their shared history.<br /><br />This is the story of the Scottish clan of Calum the Red, who came to Nova Scotia over 200 years ago. They come from that rich ancient oral tradition where the family stories are repeated endlessly through the centuries, and added to as events progress. They're all so hopelessly inbred within the clan that they can barely keep track of who's who. There are THREE Alexander MacDonalds in the story! All cousins who basically look the same, redheaded but dark eyed. Even the dogs are inbred, descended from the brown dog Calum the Red brought from Scotland. Given the confusing family ties, I thought the author did an amazing job of setting them apart so I could keep track of all the people.<br /><br />The main story takes place in modern times, with the narrator telling his story of being raised by his grandparents after his parents died when he was three years old. The behaviors and connections of the clan are so deeply rooted in the ancestral experiences that the oft-repeated histories and songs sometimes appear more real and important than current events. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2860054898637841470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_306668446" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306668446" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/306668446?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099283928" href="/book/show/609892.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=own-in-paperback">own-in-paperback</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=canadian-literature">canadian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer306668446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9364197709638532760">I just learned that Alistair MacLeod died yesterday. This shouldn't be such a shock - he was 77, and suffered a major stroke in January which forced him to remain in a hospital in Windsor, Ontario - a city where he lived and taught, and ultimately passed away. I was reading materials on him work just a few weeks ago and he was still with us, and now he's not. Despite being an acclaimed author in his native Canada and abroad, Mr. MacLeod remained a very private person - I had no idea about his co</span>
  <span id="freeText9364197709638532760" style="display:none">I just learned that Alistair MacLeod died yesterday. This shouldn't be such a shock - he was 77, and suffered a major stroke in January which forced him to remain in a hospital in Windsor, Ontario - a city where he lived and taught, and ultimately passed away. I was reading materials on him work just a few weeks ago and he was still with us, and now he's not. Despite being an acclaimed author in his native Canada and abroad, Mr. MacLeod remained a very private person - I had no idea about his condition, and the abrupt news of his death were unexpected and touched me.<br /><br /><i>No Great Mischief</i> is Alistair MacLeod's only novel - before it he was mostly known for his short stories, which were ultimately collected in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10955240.Island_The_Complete_Stories" title="Island The Complete Stories by Alistair MacLeod" rel="nofollow noopener">Island: The Complete Stories</a>. Alistair MacLeod was born in North Battleford, in the prairie province of Saskatchewan, but his fiction is set in the Canadian maritimes - mostly in Nova Scotia's Cape Breton Island, where he came to live at the age of 10. Although he later moved to Windsor and taught at the local university for over four decades, every year he returned to Cape Breton for the summer. The long history of the island, its unique culture and unrelenting weather provided plenty of inspiration for these fictions, which ultimately culminated in this book - Macleod was 64 when it was first published, and it has since won him a number of awards and honors. In 2009 was voted to be Atlantic Canada's greatest book of all time.<br /><br /><i>No Great Mischief</i> begins in the fall, my favorite season, with the narration of Alexander MacDonald, a successful orthodontist who journeys across southwestern Ontario on Highway 401, from his home in Windsor towards the city of Toronto, where he is to see Calum, his older brother. It is September, the golden month, and as Alexander notes <i>in the splendid autumn sunshine the bounty of the land is almost overwhelming, as if it is the manifestation of a poem by Keats</i> - and the drive to Toronto becomes one towards the past, as the emotional ties between brothers give way towards a narrative mirroring almost whole of Canadian history, starting from the expulsion of Calum MacDonald and his family from the Scottish Highlands in 1779 and their journey and subsequent arrival in Cape Breton, <i>the land of trees</i>, where they would form the <i>clann Chalum Ruaidh</i> - a closely knit community with its own identity and history. The title comes from general James Wolfe's assertion of the fierce Scottish Highlanders, whom he summoned to fight in the Battle of Plains of Abraham: "hey are hardy, intrepid, accustomed to a rough country, and no great mischief if they fall."<br /><br />This is - and most likely will remain - Alistair MacLeod's only novel. He was a perfectionist, and one who never rewrote his work - he wrote a sentence of a story, waited for the muses, and wrote another sentence. He also wrote the final sentence halfway through, as a light to guide him throughout it. <i>No Great Mischief</i> in both form and theme is reminiscent of the oral tradition of storytelling, when an elder would sit down in front of his audience and begin to tell his story; there aren't any wasted words here, and in its short length (under 300 pages) the novel manages to describe history of generations of people and a country. It isn't a perfect book - there are many moments where MacLeod veers too close to bathos, such as by having his characters randomly break into songs in Gaelic one too many times - and the symbolism is sometimes too obvious - there's one scene where a character walks into a room with a t-shirt saying <i>living in the past is not living up to our potential</i> - but it is a book full of genuine charm and purely human heart, a product of great effort and time, and one that I will read again. This is why, in honor of the author, I am giving it the extra fifth star - with sadness, as we will not read a story by him again. Rest in Peace, Mr. MacLeod.<br /><br /><i>'All of us are better when we're loved'</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9364197709638532760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152398222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18120585257359047438">I guess this is not my sort of book. It is the tale of Scots in Cape Breton and in particular a branch of the MacDonalds, and makes much of how they never forgot their roots, always stick together, and still speak Gaelic. It won various prizes and is considered the best Atlantic Canadian novel. But how it got so esteemed I have no idea. I found it tiresome and longwinded. There is really not much of a plot except a bunch of disjointed anecdotes. The characters are little more than mouthpieces fo</span>
  <span id="freeText18120585257359047438" style="display:none">I guess this is not my sort of book. It is the tale of Scots in Cape Breton and in particular a branch of the MacDonalds, and makes much of how they never forgot their roots, always stick together, and still speak Gaelic. It won various prizes and is considered the best Atlantic Canadian novel. But how it got so esteemed I have no idea. I found it tiresome and longwinded. There is really not much of a plot except a bunch of disjointed anecdotes. The characters are little more than mouthpieces for their ethnic background - continually making historical references (to Culloden and Wolfe) at any moment and continually reciting the same lines. It is difficult to know who is speaking or where they are speaking since they all say the same things all the time. And nothing much happens but even what does happen makes little or no impact since none of the characters seem to have any depth. One character called Alexander MacDonald gets replaced by another character called Alexander MacDonald for no particular reason. <br /><br />The traveling to Scotland falls particularly flat as everyone they meet is out of a North American fantasy of the Scottish Highlands. Immediately recognizing the MacDonalds by their red hair and black eyes and talking of the '45 and Prince Charlie as if it all happened yesterday. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18120585257359047438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer4758221536091210846">I really enjoy Alistair MacLeod’s prose, some of the best I’ve read. While the characters are not very dynamic in No Great Mischief and the life of miners and this clan from Cape Breton are not the most interesting of subjects, if nothing else this book should be savored for the beautiful writing, the nostalgia it evokes and its strong sense of place. I enjoyed Islands, MacLeod’s book of short stories, a little more because it was more colorful but No Great Mischief is very thought provoking whe</span>
  <span id="freeText4758221536091210846" style="display:none">I really enjoy Alistair MacLeod’s prose, some of the best I’ve read. While the characters are not very dynamic in No Great Mischief and the life of miners and this clan from Cape Breton are not the most interesting of subjects, if nothing else this book should be savored for the beautiful writing, the nostalgia it evokes and its strong sense of place. I enjoyed Islands, MacLeod’s book of short stories, a little more because it was more colorful but No Great Mischief is very thought provoking when it comes to old clans dispersing across North America in search of jobs which are not in supply in Cape Breton.<br /><br />4 stars. One summer, a number of years ago, I drove my family around Cape Breton in our RV. One of my fondest road trips. There is a truthfulness to MacLeod’s writing about this place. The imagery he evokes of a beautiful and maritime Cape Breton matched my observations made while driving, camping and hiking there. It’s a region more resistant to modernization than perhaps any place in Canada — some of the signs are still in Gaelic. But the roads might be the worst I’ve ever seen in a 1st world country. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4758221536091210846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_56766045" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56766045" />
    <a title="Paul Burry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2338304-paul-burry"><img alt="Paul Burry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352910924p2/2338304.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56766045?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Burry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Burry" href="/user/show/2338304-paul-burry">Paul Burry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780771055676" href="/book/show/919534.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56766045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1368442796226508367">"My sister was silent for a moment.<br/><br/>'Calum once told me,' I said, 'that when they went back to the country, they went one day to cut a timber for the skidway they were making for their boat. They went into a tightly packed grove of spruce down by the shore. In the middle of the grove, they saw what they thought was the perfect tree. It was tall and straight and over thirty feet high. They notched it as they had been taught and then they sawed it with a bucksaw. When they had sawed it completely t</span>
  <span id="freeText1368442796226508367" style="display:none">"My sister was silent for a moment.<br /><br />'Calum once told me,' I said, 'that when they went back to the country, they went one day to cut a timber for the skidway they were making for their boat. They went into a tightly packed grove of spruce down by the shore. In the middle of the grove, they saw what they thought was the perfect tree. It was tall and straight and over thirty feet high. They notched it as they had been taught and then they sawed it with a bucksaw. When they had sawed it completely through, nothing happened. The tree's upper branches were so densely intertwined with those around it that it just remained standing. There was no way it could be removed or fall unless the whole grove was cut down. It remained like that for years. Perhaps it is still there. When the wind blew, the whole grove would move and sigh. Becuase all of the trees were evergreen they never lost their foliage, and the supporting trees extended their branches every year. If you walked by the grove, Calum said, you would never realize that in its midst there was a tall straight tree that was severed at its stump.'"</span>
  <a data-text-id="1368442796226508367" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1090761428" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1090761428" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1090761428?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/18617887-no-great-mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=best-of-2014">best-of-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=canada">canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1090761428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5559599250983447206"><br/><i>
  <b>We are all better when we're loved.</b>
</i><br/><br/>1940s Cape Breton is a place untouched by modern ideas of individualism. Here identity is not forged by choices made, but by birth into a history, birth into a clan, birth into your place in that lineage that stretches back to Calum Ruadh who came from Moidart to the New World in 1779 when he was a man of 55, who lived another 55 years in the land of trees, giving his life a strange sort of balance. Who are your parents, who are your grandparents, those are</span>
  <span id="freeText5559599250983447206" style="display:none"><br /><i>
  <b>We are all better when we're loved.</b>
</i><br /><br />1940s Cape Breton is a place untouched by modern ideas of individualism. Here identity is not forged by choices made, but by birth into a history, birth into a clan, birth into your place in that lineage that stretches back to Calum Ruadh who came from Moidart to the New World in 1779 when he was a man of 55, who lived another 55 years in the land of trees, giving his life a strange sort of balance. Who are your parents, who are your grandparents, those are the questions to establish who you are. Ah, <i>clann Chalum Ruadh</i>. That is who you are.<br /><br />Our narrator is Alexander MacDonald, but he hardly knows his own name until poked by a cousin when he fails to respond to it called from the roll at school. He is <i>gille beag ruadh</i>, the little red-haired boy. Years have passed since that time; he drives from south west Ontario to visit his eldest brother Calum in Toronto, stranded there in the back lanes between Spadina Avenue and Yonge Street, a man who has nothing, a man who has everything. In majestic, sweeping rhythms, <i>'ille beag ruadh</i> interweaves the history of his family and of the MacDonalds, those same MacDonalds betrayed by the Campbells at Glencoe, those highland men who fought alongside General Wolfe on the plains of Abraham, of whom the General said "They are hardy, intrepid, accustomed to a rough country, and no great mischief if they fall." Past and present drift and mingle, indistinguishable. Is it nought but fine irony when a young woman in a black T-shirt walks towards him: the T-shirt carrying the slogan "Living in the past is not living up to our potential."?<br /><br />The men of the clan work the land and the sea, and when that no longer serves, Alexander's brothers set off to work deep under the surface in the uranium mines of Elliot Lake. When a cousin, also Alexander MacDonald, is killed in an accident, this Alexander MacDonald steps in to fill his place. He, in turn, is replaced by another cousin, another Alexander MacDonald, come from California to escape the draft. They are elements in a complex network, one that demands fierce loyalty and equally fierce adherence to a code of honour among blood. When that code is broken, Calum, the eldest, the leader, must take the rap. <br /><br />Equally as remarkable as the relations between brothers and cousins is the relationship between man and beast; even the dog who cares too much and tries too hard, even the horse who always keeps her side of the bargain, aye, even they show that fierce loyalty. <br /><br />Only the sister is a weakness in the construct of this saga, as she appears too much as a mere spokesperson, a history lesson, a lecture for our edification, but not our delight. And the ending slips just a touch into melodrama and sentiment, twin spectres at the feast, so successfully shut out from all that had gone before, although the chance for them to wheedle their way in was often there. But somehow a book has to end, and why not with a death foreseen, and a return home across a storm swept causeway. Crossing to the other side, indeed.<br /><br />Soundtrack:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=wT_Gdy4hDPQ">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=wT_Gdy...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5559599250983447206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1108942733" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1108942733" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1108942733?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21420566-no-great-mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=canada">canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1108942733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7093241597638171917"><i>"The ‘lamp of the poor’ is hardly visible in urban southwestern Ontario, although there are many poor who move disjointedly beneath it. And the stars are seldom clearly seen above the pollution of prosperity."</i><br/> <br/>This, in short, is what I liked about the book. Yes, I do mean that particular quote. <br/> <br/>I know this is one of those books that a lot of people seem to really like, and I can understand why, but for me this was a frustrating and really annoying read. To the extent that I even got annoyed wit</span>
  <span id="freeText7093241597638171917" style="display:none"><i>"The ‘lamp of the poor’ is hardly visible in urban southwestern Ontario, although there are many poor who move disjointedly beneath it. And the stars are seldom clearly seen above the pollution of prosperity."</i><br /> <br />This, in short, is what I liked about the book. Yes, I do mean that particular quote. <br /> <br />I know this is one of those books that a lot of people seem to really like, and I can understand why, but for me this was a frustrating and really annoying read. To the extent that I even got annoyed with things I would not usually pay much attention to, like "Why is the guy's Gaelic name spelled in two different ways?".<br /> <br />To paraphrase the author himself: <br /> <br /><i>"She could not help it, I suppose. Sometimes it is hard to choose or not to choose those things which bother us at the most inappropriate of times."</i><br /> <br />Anyway, No Great Mischief tells the stories of a family from when they first left Scotland for Canada in 1779 up to late 1970s/1980s (it's not really clear). There are plenty of colourful characters, plenty of stories of hardship, and an abundance of nostalgic references to Scotland - or rather one single event in Scottish history. For the most part, the references were limited to the Battle of Culloden and the Jacobite Uprising (around 1745/46). <br /> <br />And this, together with the nostalgia for anything Gaelic just really got on my nerves rather quickly.<br />Don't get me wrong I have rather a soft spot for Gaelic and I delight in watching BBC Alba sometimes just to hear it while reading the subtitles, but we're talking about a story relying on a few overused phrases and pretending as if everyone with the last name of MacDonald is fluent in it. <br /> <br />As for the Jacobite Uprising...Really, there is more to Scottish history and not everything that happened to the MacDonalds of Cape Breton in the 20th century can be blamed on or explained by a reference to an event in 1745/46.<br /> <br />Let me illustrate...<br /> <br />One of the MacDonald's relatives living in California is being drafted to fight in the Vietnam War and his parents send him to the branch of the family in Cape Breton to escape the draft. And the discussion is as follows:<br /> <br /><i>" ‘From what I understand of this war,’ he continued, ‘those people are only fighting for their own country and their own way of being. It’s hard to say they should be killed for that.’<br />‘I know,’ I said. ‘Wars touch all of us in different ways. I suppose we have been influenced by lots of wars ourselves. We are probably what we are because of the ’45. We are, ourselves , directly or indirectly the children of Culloden Moor, and what happened in its aftermath.’<br />‘Yes,’ he said with a smile, ‘the old men at home, the seanaichies, always used to say, “If only the ships had come from France …”<br />’‘Maybe,’ I said. ‘We’ll never know. Perhaps it was all questionable from the start. Talking about history is not like living it, I guess. Some people have more choice than others.’ "</i><br /> <br />Aha, yup. Culloden. Of course. Everything can be traced back to Culloden. No mention of the Union of Crowns, the bribery surrounding the Darien scheme, and the resulting Act of Union. Or why not go back further to the wars of Scottish independence?<br /> <br />Incidentally, I do get that part of the book's message is how people might be held back by living in the past - or as MacLeod puts it: <br /> <br /><i>" ‘Living in the past is not living up to our potential.’ "</i><br /> <br />It's just that this message - conveyed as a joke - is rather muddled by a lot sentimental illusion. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7093241597638171917" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41309233" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41309233" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/198932-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186690329p2/198932.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41309233?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/198932-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375726651" href="/book/show/14273.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41309233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13129515828621229717">Set in Cape Breton in the nineteen seventies, No Great Mischief revolves around the visit of a successful orthodontist to his alcoholic brother eking out a miserable existence in a sqalid room above a shop in Toronto. <br/><br/>The visit is the starting point for a narrative that follows the fortunes of a group of Scots-Canadians descended from one legendary eighteenth century immigrant. Hardy and tightly-knit in the face of recurring tragedy, the extended family see themselves and the rest of the world w</span>
  <span id="freeText13129515828621229717" style="display:none">Set in Cape Breton in the nineteen seventies, No Great Mischief revolves around the visit of a successful orthodontist to his alcoholic brother eking out a miserable existence in a sqalid room above a shop in Toronto. <br /><br />The visit is the starting point for a narrative that follows the fortunes of a group of Scots-Canadians descended from one legendary eighteenth century immigrant. Hardy and tightly-knit in the face of recurring tragedy, the extended family see themselves and the rest of the world with reference to their group identity. But it's an identity that is being eroded by modernity and the book is an elegy to the last generation of the clan to understand itself in this way. <br /><br />The robust storytelling is woven around with imagery that explores the book's central themes of leadership, community, intransigence and betrayal. Poignant and intelligently written, this is a book quite unlike much contemporary fiction in that its concerns are not only with the personal but also with community.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13129515828621229717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15416760679753207251">I really enjoyed this book -- much more than I'd expected to from reading the description. I know very little about Canada's history or even its geography, so I actually found myself occasionally consulting a map to locate the relevant places from the text. The writing was beautiful and managed to be sentimental without being sappy or sarcastic. I'd recommend this book to anyone looking for a powerful read that sneaks up on you as you're going along.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2033430723908762328">I absolutely loved this book. I would recommend this book to anyone, especially if they are descendents from Scotland or Ireland. It deals with the struggles that people have trying to hold onto their roots, yet becoming a part of a new society. It's the old world vs. the new world struggle. Some embrace the "modern" world and leave their family and their legacy to be part of this world, while others desperately hold onto whatever heritage they have left and forfeit a lot to do it.<br/><br/>Another aspect</span>
  <span id="freeText2033430723908762328" style="display:none">I absolutely loved this book. I would recommend this book to anyone, especially if they are descendents from Scotland or Ireland. It deals with the struggles that people have trying to hold onto their roots, yet becoming a part of a new society. It's the old world vs. the new world struggle. Some embrace the "modern" world and leave their family and their legacy to be part of this world, while others desperately hold onto whatever heritage they have left and forfeit a lot to do it.<br /><br />Another aspect deals with a person's connection to their geography. A common thread in Canadian literature deals with nature's unwillingness to change to man's desire. For example, a plowed field will once again create weeds and take over again. It's a constant battle between man and nature.<br /><br />This book is well written and well thought out. Worthy of reading.</span>

<div id="review_628951595" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/628951595" />
    <a title="Laurel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15977158-laurel"><img alt="Laurel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357070199p2/15977158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/628951595?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laurel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laurel" href="/user/show/15977158-laurel">Laurel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780771055706" href="/book/show/919530.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15977158-laurel?shelf=across-canada-challenge">across-canada-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15977158-laurel?shelf=canadian-authors">canadian-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15977158-laurel?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15977158-laurel?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer628951595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9610633588548627669">I read this book, quickly, in less than twenty-four hours. It really held my attention, and I was interested to see how it ended. This novel won several major literary prizes when it was published in 1999 by Alistair Macleod, a Canadian writer. The narrator is an orthodontist, who frequently visits his alcoholic older brother in a rundown rooming house on downtown Toronto. These visits provide the opportunity for the narrator, Alexander MacDonald, to tell the story of his family's history in Can</span>
  <span id="freeText9610633588548627669" style="display:none">I read this book, quickly, in less than twenty-four hours. It really held my attention, and I was interested to see how it ended. This novel won several major literary prizes when it was published in 1999 by Alistair Macleod, a Canadian writer. The narrator is an orthodontist, who frequently visits his alcoholic older brother in a rundown rooming house on downtown Toronto. These visits provide the opportunity for the narrator, Alexander MacDonald, to tell the story of his family's history in Canada, the clan of Calum the Red, dating back to their settlement in Cape Breton, Nova Sçotia, in 1779.<br /><br />The present day members of the narrator's clan are forever ever linked to all Macdonalds of previous generations, proudly tracing their roots back to Scotland. The stories of how different generations coped with living in the "land of the trees"' beside the sea, are poignantly interwoven into the narrator' s present day task of visiting his brother Calum, an ex-con and a drunk, with the events of his childhood, and the relationships between himself, his twin sister, the grandparents who raised them and the multitudes of cousins and other relations who had a hand in his upbringing as a member of the clan. Music, oral history, homesteading in a difficult natural environment, uranium mining in Elliott Lake, and present day cultural influences are all tied together to suggest that regardless of where you go, you frequently take the past that has moulded you into your modern self, with you. <br /><br />Macleod's writing style is somewhat sparse, yet poetic. He excels at creating vivid images in your mind's eye. I could visualize the scenes in the old kitchen, with members of the family gathered round, listening to a grandfather's story passed from generation to generation, with dogs panting on the floor, and whiskey or beer being passed around. I was down in that terrible uranium mine, feeling the silica and water running out of drenched gloves as Alex and his brother drilled into the tough Canadian Shield for 12 hours a day, since there was limited work to be found at home. How many folks from the east, have travelled great distances to find work, in our present day? A common thread and link to the past. Macleod is really adept at describing many examples like this one, making the past relevant.<br /><br />I have yet to visit Nova Scotia, but will certainly think of the traditions and pride, the courage and steadfastness of the clans who settled Cape Breton, and whose relatives strive to keep their Celtic heritage alive, when I visit the land of the trees. I was moved by the story Macleod wove, and thought that his narrator' s grandfather, likely shared some of the qualities of my dad's parents. Around 1900, they tried valiantly to homestead in the northern reaches of Saskatchewan-without a doctor to birth three children or nurse a family through a long bout of scarlet fever. There were deadly blizzards when you could die only several yards from your cabin or barn, you lost hold of the life saving rope strung from cabin to barn through the long winter.The clan suffered its own winter tragedy, which had an enormous impact on the entire family, but particularly the narrator. To say more would lessen the impact, and deprive the reader of a crucial experience, and undermine the author's plan for the reader. I think reading this book brought me closer to my own past. That alone, makes it worthwhile.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9610633588548627669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_393596547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/393596547" />
    <a title="Sharon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5934145-sharon"><img alt="Sharon" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/393596547?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sharon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharon" href="/user/show/5934145-sharon">Sharon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375726651" href="/book/show/14273.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer393596547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17969166619151188204">Alistair MacLeod doesn't waste a word as he tells the story of a fiercely loyal family bound by shared history and culture even as they move through tragedy after tragedy to make their way in the "new country." <br/><br/>The story is told through the eyes of Alexander MacDonald, orphaned as a child by a terrible tragedy and raised by his grandparents. Repetition of phrases, proverbs and themes, juxtaposition of current and past circumstances, reflection on the MacDonald clan's past tragic history, musings</span>
  <span id="freeText17969166619151188204" style="display:none">Alistair MacLeod doesn't waste a word as he tells the story of a fiercely loyal family bound by shared history and culture even as they move through tragedy after tragedy to make their way in the "new country." <br /><br />The story is told through the eyes of Alexander MacDonald, orphaned as a child by a terrible tragedy and raised by his grandparents. Repetition of phrases, proverbs and themes, juxtaposition of current and past circumstances, reflection on the MacDonald clan's past tragic history, musings about the irony of trying to determine "which of us were the lucky ones" as each new family tragedy unfolds -- these and other literary devices powerfully impact the reader and convey the way in which members of this clan are sustained by a family history that lives on within them. Throughout the book runs a hauntingly beautiful Gaelic refrain, binding the family together across generations, regardless of modernization or the dementia of old age.<br /><br />Reading this book while in a Cape Breton to visit my husband's MacDonald family made the reading of this book especially meaningful for me. MacLeod captures perfectly the culture of the island and the struggle to hold on to its unique identity and ancestral culture in the face of modernization and so many of its young people "heading west."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17969166619151188204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14521802" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3740644943581117538">This was a Christmas gift from my mom, and it is also one that I would have bought for myself.<br/>It won several international awards, and the back cover and inside pages are lush with glowing reviews from across the literary landscape of esteemed writers and reviewers.<br/>(You can see where I'm going with this, can't you?)<br/><br/>It's a story that roots itself, for the most part, in my birthplace, Cape Breton, Nova Scotia And, it's a novelization about the MacDonald clan! This novel had so much going for it fo</span>
  <span id="freeText3740644943581117538" style="display:none">This was a Christmas gift from my mom, and it is also one that I would have bought for myself.<br />It won several international awards, and the back cover and inside pages are lush with glowing reviews from across the literary landscape of esteemed writers and reviewers.<br />(You can see where I'm going with this, can't you?)<br /><br />It's a story that roots itself, for the most part, in my birthplace, Cape Breton, Nova Scotia And, it's a novelization about the MacDonald clan! This novel had so much going for it for me before I even cracked the cover open that it pains me now to write a disappointed review.<br />Not that it was that bad, but because it wasn't that great. There were some nice observances and some good imagery, but I don't know, the novel as a whole just had a certain blandness to it and didn't really do a whole lot for me. <br />It was short, so not a lot of time to devote to it so sure, give it a shot if you want. Who knows, you just may respond like the Globe and Mail reviewer who called this the book of the decade. Funny, I seem to find all "great writers (Ondaatje, etc.)" terribly bland and uninteresting. Maybe ahm jus' stoopid.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1806546495671921952">A friend recommended (and dropped off) this book for me to read. In contemplating my review, I've just decided to (word-for-word) hash out my text message to her on my feelings toward "No Great Mischief." That way I don't have to re-think my feelings for this book a second time.<br/><br/>Conversation:<br/>Friend: Did you like it? (Don't be afraid to tell me your true thought).<br/>Me: "I thought the book was alright, but definitely not a favorite. Which slightly surprised me because this was a historical fiction no</span>
  <span id="freeText1806546495671921952" style="display:none">A friend recommended (and dropped off) this book for me to read. In contemplating my review, I've just decided to (word-for-word) hash out my text message to her on my feelings toward "No Great Mischief." That way I don't have to re-think my feelings for this book a second time.<br /><br />Conversation:<br />Friend: Did you like it? (Don't be afraid to tell me your true thought).<br />Me: "I thought the book was alright, but definitely not a favorite. Which slightly surprised me because this was a historical fiction novel and those are my favorite! I got annoyed with all the Gaelic words and references. And I felt that the author didn't explain the war very well so I had a hard time with all the references to the war and what it was trying to allude to. I hated that the author used grandfather to describe one paternal grandparent and grandfather to describe the other maternal grandparent. It was extremely confusing and I didn't figure it out until I was 2/3 through the book. I also had a hard time understanding the "point" the author was trying to carry across. The back of the book was captivating and I was excited to read more and more to find out about this "murder" that takes place. But it didn't happen until 10-20 pages from the end. That was frustrating and I felt that the author could have done a lot more to develop that plot or idea. So overall, I guess, it was okay. Sorry the review was a little harsh, but I'm only reading for an hour in the middle of the night... So I'm moody at this time and need a book with more guts."<br /><br />And... yup... I think that summarizes my feelings for this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1806546495671921952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14206696964870789967">This is the second time I've read this book, and I was teary-eyed at the end again. It's the story of the clann Chalum Ruaidh, or the Clan of Calum the Red, an 18th century Scottish Highlander who crossed the ocean to Nova Scotia. His family, the following clann, has to come to terms with the fact that their somewhat famous ancestor crossed the ocean to start a new life with his family...to what end? <br/><br/>This is an absorbing novel, and MacLeod is an incredibly mature writer. With anyone else, it mig</span>
  <span id="freeText14206696964870789967" style="display:none">This is the second time I've read this book, and I was teary-eyed at the end again. It's the story of the clann Chalum Ruaidh, or the Clan of Calum the Red, an 18th century Scottish Highlander who crossed the ocean to Nova Scotia. His family, the following clann, has to come to terms with the fact that their somewhat famous ancestor crossed the ocean to start a new life with his family...to what end? <br /><br />This is an absorbing novel, and MacLeod is an incredibly mature writer. With anyone else, it might have seemed to drag. But MacLeod has the ability to weave the story so that the reader isn't necessarily concerned with moving forward and progressing through the plot as she is concerned with penetrating the family history, Scottish history, and Nova Scotian history in general. <br /><br />This is a lovely, lovely book. It's something that stays with you long after you finish it.</span>

  <span id="freeText10287939336188238179" style="display:none">I thought this was one of the most poorly written books that I've ever read. And the constant repetition of the clan’s name and that they had red or black hair and the fact that the moon was the “lamp of the poor” was unbearable.</span>

<div id="review_3224056023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3224056023" />
    <a title="Nick Munene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90921615-nick-munene"><img alt="Nick Munene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590632408p2/90921615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3224056023?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Munene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Munene" href="/user/show/90921615-nick-munene">Nick Munene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3224056023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5002579865785640164">I enjoyed reading this novel but the first few chapters felt like they needed one more careful edit before publishing. However, the narrative pulled me in especially the struggles in the mines and both grandfathers. I liked Calum best and reread the first few chapters just to get a glimpse of him again and I feel like despite his many shortcomings, life dealt him unfair cards. My best moments were always Grandpa's drunken musings and the phalic imagery that followed. <br/><br/>In today's campaigns for min</span>
  <span id="freeText5002579865785640164" style="display:none">I enjoyed reading this novel but the first few chapters felt like they needed one more careful edit before publishing. However, the narrative pulled me in especially the struggles in the mines and both grandfathers. I liked Calum best and reread the first few chapters just to get a glimpse of him again and I feel like despite his many shortcomings, life dealt him unfair cards. My best moments were always Grandpa's drunken musings and the phalic imagery that followed. <br /><br />In today's campaigns for minority empowerment and talks around straight, white man privilege, this book portrays clearly how this sort of characterization is not shared across the board; it's a preserve for the very wealthy straight white men. Switch up the Scottish last names for a minority's last name and the devastating realities of the downtrodden are clearly shown; that they transcend even race and ethnicity. So, in the same ships that brought enterprising Scottish merchants to the New World who made a fortune in the fur trade and the Hudson's Bay Company, others ended up poor up to their next generations with little prospects for advancement due to the labour exploitation of mining companies like Renco Development. No wonder things turned out the way they did for poor Calum who is now an alcoholic excon struggling in Toronto! <br /><br />The tension between French Canadians and other Canadians is poignantly captured when their draft dodger cousin from SF compares them to Mexicans insisting that they should only speak in English since they're in North America. How ridiculous! <br /><br />This is a great piece which needed more polishing up before presentation. I've read that MacLeod was a perfectionist who wrote sentence after sentence as they came to him without altering previous phrases. Hopefully in my next great short stories beinge, I'll remember to sample a few of his especially if he draws forth the tremendous beauty of Cape Breton.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5002579865785640164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_917224589" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/917224589" />
    <a title="Mary Soderstrom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10505847-mary-soderstrom"><img alt="Mary Soderstrom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488296517p2/10505847.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/917224589?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary Soderstrom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary Soderstrom" href="/user/show/10505847-mary-soderstrom">Mary Soderstrom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer917224589" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2605052405607717030"> Alistair MacLoed Is Dead: Far from No Great Mischief<br/>The news this weekend is that Alistair MacLeod, short story writer and novelist, is dead at 77. As it happened, we had a very stimulating discussion of his novel No Great Mischief on Wednesday at the Atwater Library. Most of the participants found much of interest in the book, although our resident dentist said the episode where one of the characters ties a rope around a tooth in order to pull it far from realistic.<br/><br/><br/><br/>The book, which MacLeod work</span>
  <span id="freeText2605052405607717030" style="display:none"> Alistair MacLoed Is Dead: Far from No Great Mischief<br />The news this weekend is that Alistair MacLeod, short story writer and novelist, is dead at 77. As it happened, we had a very stimulating discussion of his novel No Great Mischief on Wednesday at the Atwater Library. Most of the participants found much of interest in the book, although our resident dentist said the episode where one of the characters ties a rope around a tooth in order to pull it far from realistic.<br /><br /><br /><br />The book, which MacLeod worked on for 13 years before finally agreeing to have it typed up and prepared for publication by MacLelland and Stewart in 1999, is told by a Cape Breton man (one of three Alexander MacDonalds in the novel, now living a nice upper middle class life in Windsor, ON, as he tries ot help his alcoholic older brother. The pace is slow, as it often is when stories are told aloud. There is repetition of key phrases and repetition of key events, just as there is in stories told aloud. The voice is easy, as if the man narrating the events was sure from the beginning how it would end.<br /><br />Along the way we are told a lot about the Highland Scots who settled in Cape Breton in the late 18th century, and how their progeny continue today to eke out an existence on the rough landscape. It is a moving story, and one which I found absorbing.<br /><br />As we spoke about the book last week, I wondered just how much of my appreciation of the book is colored by the fact that my mother was a McDonald, from the Protestant part of that clan. Certainly the people who seemed most taken by the book had a link with Scotland one way or another. But MacLeod goes to great lengths to make the connection between the clannishness of the Highland Scots and the ties that bind peole in other communities: French Canadians, Zulus, the Masai, Mexican Mennonites.<br /><br />Indeed, the link between the Scots and the French in North America is one of the major themes in the book, and led to an almost surreal book launch when the French translation came out a couple of years after English original. The event was sponsored by the Quebec nationalist Société St-Jean Baptiste at its headquarters in an elegant old house once owned by the Patriot Ludger Duvernay. I suppose I was invited because I'd written a fictionalized biography of another of the Patriots (and here we're talking about the Rebellions in both Lower and Upper Canada in 1837-38) Robert Nelson. When guests arrived we were greeted by two men in kilts playing bagpipes outside. Inside we were greated to Scottish dancing, a short talk by MacLeod (in English, since he spoke no French), a reading of the French translation and the presentation of a scholarly work about the many French Quebeckers who have Scottish ancestors.<br /><br />The leaders of the SJB society at the time were following very carefully the secession movement in Scotland, and had read MacLeod's book in English on their way to attend the first session of the Scottish assembly. Obviously they found a great many resonances with the Quebec situation. As for me, I was delighted by the connections they made, as well as the pipers.<br /><br />Will pipers play Amazing Grace or Over the Sea to Skye at MacLeod's funeral which will be held this Saturday at St. Margaret of Scotland Church at Broad Cove, Cape Breton? Possibly. A more interesting question is: how religious will the service be? One of the things that is strangely missing from No Great Mischief is organized religion. MacLeod avoids any reference to the Catholic-Protestant cleavage in Scottish culture in his books, because, I'd like to think, that conflict is irrelevant when considering the relations of people to nature and to the hard scrabble life so many live. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2605052405607717030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1023599094" />
    <a title="Anita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29405146-anita"><img alt="Anita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407269712p2/29405146.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1023599094?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anita" href="/user/show/29405146-anita">Anita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1023599094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13072579301214239429">This haunting story stays with you long after you have put down the book.<br/>It is beautifully written, the descriptions of the settings are so evocative that I actually felt as if I had visited Cape Breton myself.<br/>It tells the story of a family, the MacDonalds, who leave Scotland in the late 18th Century to journey to Canada, and it follows the lives of their ancestors in the Cape Breton area of Nova Scotia. Most of the MacDonald clan intermarry, so that they form a very tight-knit community, who re</span>
  <span id="freeText13072579301214239429" style="display:none">This haunting story stays with you long after you have put down the book.<br />It is beautifully written, the descriptions of the settings are so evocative that I actually felt as if I had visited Cape Breton myself.<br />It tells the story of a family, the MacDonalds, who leave Scotland in the late 18th Century to journey to Canada, and it follows the lives of their ancestors in the Cape Breton area of Nova Scotia. Most of the MacDonald clan intermarry, so that they form a very tight-knit community, who remain fiercely loyal to their kin.<br />Most of the characters are finely drawn. I especially loved the Grandma, the two Grandfathers and Calum the eldest grandson; also the various animals owned by the MacDonalds in the story. I grew to love Cindy, the horse, and the family dogs, loyal to their deaths. The only character who, to me, remained shadowy was the granddaughter, twin to the narrator, Alexander MacDonald. He is one of three Alexander MacDonalds in the story, which the author cleverly distinguishes from one another, so that the reader does not become confused.<br />The only other criticism I offer is the fact that the book contains a lot of history of the Scottish Highlands, with which many readers will be unfamiliar. There are also many Gaelic songs and expressions. <br />This is a story of courage in the face of hardship, set in an unforgiving environment. Those who have left their homeland to find a "better life" often discover that life is not any better in their New Land. <br />It is a sad tale of loss and the unpredictability of life, but there are amusing moments especially given Grandpa's antics when inebriated. <br />Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13072579301214239429" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2953108365" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2953108365" />
    <a title="Claire (Silver Linings and Pages)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84617572-claire-silver-linings-and-pages"><img alt="Claire (Silver Linings and Pages)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549021608p2/84617572.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2953108365?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire (Silver Linings and Pages)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire (Silver Linings and Pages)" href="/user/show/84617572-claire-silver-linings-and-pages">Claire (Silver Linings and Pages)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099283928" href="/book/show/609892.No_Great_Mischief">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2953108365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7666982260606181239">This novel is quiet but mighty; I loved it.<br/>.<br/>No Great Mischief is narrated by successful orthodontist, Alexander MacDonald, who visits his ailing alcoholic brother in Toronto. The narrative is woven with their family memories, and the saga of their ancestor Calum MacDonald, who left the Scottish Highlands in 1779 and settled in Nova Scotia with his family.<br/>.<br/>It’s so difficult to describe this special book. Plot-wise, not much happens and yet everything happens because it is about life events in a s</span>
  <span id="freeText7666982260606181239" style="display:none">This novel is quiet but mighty; I loved it.<br />.<br />No Great Mischief is narrated by successful orthodontist, Alexander MacDonald, who visits his ailing alcoholic brother in Toronto. The narrative is woven with their family memories, and the saga of their ancestor Calum MacDonald, who left the Scottish Highlands in 1779 and settled in Nova Scotia with his family.<br />.<br />It’s so difficult to describe this special book. Plot-wise, not much happens and yet everything happens because it is about life events in a small community. It focuses on all the important things, and particularly family loyalty - “blood is thicker than water”. It also deals with ever relevant themes such as people displacement, identity, culture, and the importance of passing on legacy through storytelling, music and language. It is so powerful and moving in how it highlights the transcience of life, and how we deal with death, in both an emotional and cultural sense.<br />.<br />Some weeks after reading, I’m still affected by the story and thinking about it. The soulful, lyrical prose is brimming with striking symbolism, and beautifully evokes the “physical and mental geography”. It is such an atmospheric, elegiac and tender book that whilst reading, I was transported back to places I’ve visited and experiences in my life. My lasting feeling is of a very compassionate and humane novel, with a strong theme of forgiveness:<br />“All of us are better when we’re loved.”<br />.<br />Profound and haunting. One of my favourite books of 2019 👌</span>
  <a data-text-id="7666982260606181239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YEpAhLJZlfxBTANEXUkH7xokH56O0dKjIcq+aghZJfPTKYS08FxUdFu5uG6QqXOo8TLhHY6RlBaaRd0YFJW4LA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DBRRQGPh/mjuf+f379+MrKv4L2kJNBR3Nz6Wt5oAOpq/d5VwIeQ/4PSKXN0iP/jrQO7R6gl0UsKMsfXFhsynRQ==')})
  }

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40611298-the-colony-of-unrequited-dreams"><img alt="The Colony of Unrequited Dreams" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1529680188l/40611298._SX98_.jpg" />
